030758836X|3.0|Reading GONE GIRL by Gillian Flynn was a bit like trying to eat an entire Hickory Farms summer sausage by yourself  You know the one; unidentified chunks of greasy meat and fat encased in a slimy skin that leave a lardy coating and garlicky tang in your mouth.  As you prepare to cut into it, you almost want to blow grits.  But then a small piece, followed by another, and then another and before you know it the obnoxious tube is gone, and it didn't taste half bad.  So it was for me with this sausage of a book.<br /><br />I didn't particularly like it but it grew on me the more I read.  Trying to make sense of my ambiguity has posed a real problem as I try to critique it.  The writing is good.  In fact, it's very good.  I didn't have trouble following the story.  The dialogue is crisp and vivid.  The characters are well developed and, for the most part, I understand the storyline.  But here's the problem.  The premise is so far out there that I don't believe a word of it.  The main characters, Nick and Amy Dunne, are such dimwits with so much self-absorption that they wouldn't be able to mentally function in any world that I know of.<br /><br />The story revolves around Nick and Amy's hatred so much that neither would be capable of performing the acts attributed to them by the author.  The acts themselves are so convoluted and malicious that a reasonable person would scoff at their very premise.  A pair of panties here, a bloody puppet leg there, a purse in the street, playthings with a sleeping man's fingerprints planted on them, a false pregnancy test with another woman's urine, a partially burned diary, and on and on.  No one, especially a psychopath, would ever be able to assemble all the elements Flynn has stuffed into her book into a believable chain of events..<br /><br />The early part of the book was especially difficult to digest; something like the first slices of the sausage.  Amy's diary entries were particularly troubling.  Who writes a diary like that?Page after page of literary musing and specific detail that covers many years is not credible.  Short scribbled entries with large time gaps are more normal.  Of course, as the story progresses, the need for such a detailed diary is revealed.<br /><br />I must confess that my curiosity as to how it all ends, how the vitriol is expunged, who will emerge victorious, who will become scum, and how all the shenanigans will be played out kept me in the book until the end (an ending that was as preposterous as the rest of the story).<br /><br />I recommend this book to those with tolerance for graphic language, the urge to punish someone, admiration for characters with mean spirits, and those who like sausage.<br /><br />Schuyler T Wallace<br />Author of TIN LIZARD TALES

Natalia Kills has delivered a pop album on par with the best of the past decade, as the album develops its own identity with each passing track. So refreshing to not have to hear a guest rapper on every track while the female artist tries to slip innuendo into the lyrics. Natalia Kills just lays it out there, and it shows in her performances. This album deserves to be a huge hit.

When I stumbled upon the book recently at a used book sale, it seemed to make sense to read the original source material.<br /><br />The main character of THE BOY IN THE STRIPED PAJAMAS is actually not the title character but Bruno, a nine year old German boy living in Berlin.  Bruno is dismayed to be told one day that the entire family is leaving their beautiful, five-story home--with the sliding bannister from top to bottom--to go to a desolate, isolated house where he has no other boys with whom he can play.  Bruno mourns the loss of his old life, blames his father (whose job precipitated the move to the strange place called \\"Out-With\\"), and tries to avoid his dreaded older sister.<br /><br />One day (not until about halfway through this short, 215-page novel), Bruno finally decides to explore the grounds surrounding his new home, including the area along the fence he can see from his bedroom window and the mysterious people in pajamas he observes beyond it.  At the fence he meets another boy, Schmuel, who is exactly his age.  To Bruno's surprise, Schmuel was also cruelly forced to leave his home and to be relocated to this desolate new place, although Bruno can hardly believe the details of Schmuel's stories, such as being forced to live in a single room with eleven people.  Still, Bruno's growing friendship with Schmuel helps him to forget his own unhappiness.<br /><br />Author John Boyne writes this book mainly as the tale of a young boy's loss and discovery of new friendship.  His larger commentaries are subtle, and that's all that they need to be, because of course we--the reader--know the truer, fuller story.  As a result, the writing is very effective, completely engrossing, and highly moving.  This simple story may take you by surprise; I highly recommend it.

His final solo on \\"For Whom The Bell Tolls\\" seems to end abruptly, and his opening solo for \\"Creeping Death\\" is off in the effect/tone. There isn't a lot of experimentation to be found, which I think most fans will appreciate.  The closest things I can offer is that they play \\"Nothing Else Matters\\" at a slightly faster pace that gives it more of an urgent delivery.<br /><br />Here's what it comes down to.  If you are a Metallica fan interested in a well-done live album that spans the history of the band, you need this album.  It's a 16-song journey that you will enjoy. B003K15IF8|3.0|This is a classic novel that I only recently discovered, oddly enough in my local public library's \\"new\\" section. Well, it was new to me even though it was written in 1965 and takes place in the early part of the 20th Century.<br /><br />I find Stoner a difficult book to review, because I can't truly say I enjoyed most of it - yet I can't deny that it has admirable qualities. Perhaps the highest compliment I can pay it is that John Williams gives one of the purest description of the stoic philosophy ever conceived in a novel. The protagonist, William Stoner, has a life that starts out difficult and mostly proceeds from one disappointment to the next.<br /><br />He has an unhappy marriage and encounters constant obstacles at his job as a professor. Yet through it all, he maintains a quiet dignity that could be considered heroic. Yet I have very mixed feelings about this. Stoner's passivity and stoicism can just as easily be considered weakness. Why, for example, does he remain married to a woman who only makes his life miserable?<br /><br />Speaking of his wife, Edith, I found her character to be less than fully developed. She is close to being a caricature of a female character from a Victorian novel with her moodiness, constant minor illnesses and a complete inability to communicate in a normal manner. Her motives are unclear, starting with why she marries Stoner in the first place. It's obviously not for his status or wealth as she comes from a banking family and he is a newly hired instructor at a rural college.<br /><br />The other strange related to Edith is that the point of view switches to her character briefly a couple of times for no apparent reason. This, however, does little to make the reader understand what makes her tick. As I read some of these scenes, i wondered if Williams didn't create Edith as a composite of several women who caused him grief. William at least develops a happier relationship with his daughter Grace, but even this ends up causing him heartache as Edith plots to keep the two apart. I found the latter to be one of the book's most inexplicable points. First we see that Edith has no interest at all in her daughter. Then, apparently out of pure spite, she starts spending more time with her just to prevent William from enjoying her company. Edith seems to exist only to make William unhappy.<br /><br />When it comes to William Stoner's love affair, this is presented as a hopeless romance (again, typical in Victorian and turn-of-the-century English novels) where the couple is thwarted by society's conventions. As we see both Stoner and his lover accept their fate we can either sympathize with their fatalism or wish they had more spirit. At the same time, Williams  describes this affair in lyrical and (for once) uplifting prose and convinces us that it is the highlight of Stoner's life.<br /><br />Of course, we have to read Stoner with an understanding that it was written almost 50 years ago and takes place almost a century ago. Most people in that age didn't have the sense of personal freedom than many people have today. Still, some of the characters in the novel seem like little more than stereotypes. William Stoner's parents, for example, are so taciturn and non-expressive that they barely speak.<br /><br />What I liked best about Stoner was the way it portrayed university life, including the petty politics and personal conflicts that are an inevitable part of such institutions. Whereas I found Edith's hostility towards William a little baffling, I found his conflict with his academic rival Lomax interesting and believable. Fragile egos and jealousy are common features of universities, as the author no doubt knew from personal experience.<br /><br />Even Stoner's more mundane teaching experiences are described in an interesting manner. We get a sense of the challenge it must be for even the most dedicated teacher to convey his knowledge and enthusiasm to often disinterested students.<br /><br />All in all, I had mixed feelings about Stoner. It's certainly a well written and often compelling novel. I found some of the characters and situations a little cliched, but that may partly be due to a modern bias. The largely depressing mood of the book also made it a little hard to enjoy, but it wasn't meant to be a lighthearted novel. Ultimately, I think your reaction to Stoner will depend on how much you admire the quality of stoicism.l in all, I had mixed feelings about Stoner. It's certainly a well written and often compelling novel. I found some of the characters and situations a little cliched, but that may partly be due to a modern bias. The largely depressing mood of the book also made it a little hard to enjoy, but it wasn't meant to be a lighthearted novel. Ultimately, I think your reaction to Stoner will depend on how much you admire the quality of stoicism.

1476727651|5.0|Stephen King's commercial success resulted in his work splitting into many streams: short stories, novels, movies, comics, television shows, children's books, off-beat collaborations, endless series and a variety of self-referential experiments. While I appreciate the energy and risk-taking behind Stephen Kingopolis, most of it doesn't work for me. I enjoy King's ability to tell stories and to inspire other creative people. I like the fiendish cleverness and economy of the [[ASIN:0451168615 classic short stories]] and the luxurious pace of the canonical novels. I appreciate how some of the stories underlie wonderful creative works by some of the best film and television directors. But like folk art, King's writing works for its simple virtues and, in my opinion anyway, fails when it tries to support sophisticated, big budget projects.<br /><br />Dr. Sleep will remind fans from the 1970s of the simple pleasures of unvarnished King. Of course that's literally true in the references to [[ASIN:0307743659 The Shining]] (1977) but also traces of classics like [[ASIN:0345805879 Carrie]], [[ASIN:0307743675 'Salem's Lot]] and [[ASIN:0307947300 The Stand]] (there's also some stuff that feels lifted from [[ASIN:038076363X Lawrence Block]] of the same era). The book has a beginning that doesn't feel like it was written last, one that sets up the story and gets reader and author on the same page. It has an ending too, not just a kill pretty much everyone and leave the rest directionless because the publisher ran out of paper. In between is a plot (admittedly somewhat thin, but strong enough to carry the characters and action, and fair to the reader). In short, we have here a traditional story, done exceptionally well. It will appeal to old school Stephen King fans like when an overexposed, commercialized rock band throws away the bars, the cars and the wars, unplugs their instruments and plays small clubs.<br /><br />I don't know whether it will please the broader fan base who thinks the novels are too long and the movies are better than the books and the television shows are best of all. So if you're not an old fan, don't start with this book. I also don't know if Dr. Sleep ranks with the best of King's novels. On one hand, it has a slightly derivative feel, like King got the idea of the sequel and then tried to figure out how to write it. Some things that were fresh in 1977 are tired today, and some of the new stuff included to jazz things up don't mesh. On the other hand, there's a depth and maturity to replace the iconoclastic brashness that has been lost. To an English professor, this could be King's best novel, at least to the kind of professor who thinks [[ASIN:1461035937 The Tempest]] is Shakespeare's best play.<br /><br />Definitely get this if you are a classic King fan. If you don't know whether you are a classic King fan, get one of his first six novels and find out. If you're a non-classic King fan, or don't like King, this is not the book for you.hinks the novels are too long and the movies are better than the books and the television shows are best of all. So if you're not an old fan, don't start with this book. I also don't know if Dr. Sleep ranks with the best of King's novels. On one hand, it has a slightly derivative feel, like King got the idea of the sequel and then tried to figure out how to write it. Some things that were fresh in 1977 are tired today, and some of the new stuff included to jazz things up don't mesh. On the other hand, there's a depth and maturity to replace the iconoclastic brashness that has been lost. To an English professor, this could be King's best novel, at least to the kind of professor who thinks [[ASIN:1461035937 The Tempest]] is Shakespeare's best play.<br /><br />Definitely get this if you are a classic King fan. If you don't know whether you are a classic King fan, get one of his first six novels and find out. If you're a non-classic King fan, or don't like King, this is not the book for you.

1476727651|5.0|UPDATE: The following paragraph is an update to my original review.<br /><br />My review has received a lot of votes for being unhelpful. I would like to clarify that when comparing Doctor Sleep to The Shining on a scare-by-scare basis, Doctor Sleep has more of them. When reading Doctor Sleep, you may not feel the same way you did when reading The Shining. Don't expect that--that was over thirty years ago. If you take this book at face value, there's plenty to creep you out and appreciate. If you are a fan of American Horror Story or the original book of The Shining, then by all means read this book; you'll enjoy it. If there's still something about this review that doesn't help you, then please let me know; it's the only way I can improve. And as always: thanks for reading!<br /><br />********Original Review Below*******************<br /><br />More than a scary story, I find Doctor Sleep seeping with autobiographical allegory. Over thirty years ago, we met our protagonist as he was struggling with his unique and special ability. Under the sole accompaniment of his mother, he had to leave the area where he witnessed great tragedy. Three decades later we meet him again, now living in New England. Our protagonist spent that time seeking further understanding of his supernatural gift, often turning to the dependency on the bottle. And here I leave you to wonder, whether I am referring Danny, the book's protagonist, or King the author thereof. Whether by accident or purpose, I am unsure, but the allegorical beauty remains the same.<br /><br />King has always been a great writer, gifted if you will. His has panache for telling a story, even from the beginning of his career. However, I've noticed a difference since his accident. Maybe it's my imagination or maybe it's a new meaning on life that King found, but his writing seems more clear and agile. At times it does get over-wordy (I'm talking to you Under the Dome), but it's not nearly as ambiguous as some of his earlier works. I find Doctor Sleep to be the culmination of everything King has been trying to become and his own triumphant statement over addiction and pain.<br /><br />The scares are definitely here. Hell, King starts right out with a return of that damn purple lady from the Overlook. But King has a lot to overcome in this story, mostly in conquering the faded memories of his fans. Remember those twin girls calling out for Danny to play with them? Yeah, they weren't in the book--they were from the movie. King rekindles the elements of The Shining (he does so from the very first sentence when he refers to the fire--book, not the movie) and adding elements that are sure to scare the modern reader. I liken many of these new scary moments to what you would find in the television show American Horror Story: creepy and surreal.<br /><br />Besides Danny, aka Doctor Sleep (remember him being called Doc in the original book?) we have a kick-ass girl hero named Abra (like abracadabra) and a group of country-travelling old people acting as the villains. Trust me on this one, they are creepy. On the outside they are your grandmas in the check-out line counting coupons and asking for the senior citizen discounts, or your grandpas wearing funny shirts embarrassing you on vacation, but this is their disguise to do their haunted bidding.<br /><br />By the end of the book, you'll really like Danny, but you'll really, really like Abra. There are plenty of scary scenes, such as the book's trailer indicates--spoons on ceilings; a boy being tortured far away--but the true magic, for lack of better term, happens in the battles of the astral plane. Whether it is Abra upon her dreamesque horse or Danny inside her mind, you'll be turning pages quickly to the end. Yeah, it's a little weird, but isn't that what you've been asking for?<br /><br />Speaking of the ending, many have complained of King's sudden and unexplained endings of his prior works. As another testament to his improvement, Doctorworks. I find Doctor Sleep to be the culmination of everything King has been trying to become and his own triumphant statement over addiction and pain.<br /><br />The scares are definitely here. Hell, King starts right out with a return of that damn purple lady from the Overlook. But King has a lot to overcome in this story, mostly in conquering the faded memories of his fans. As another testament to his improvement, DoctorSleep wraps up in a complete and even a bit predictable manner.<br /><br />Overall, there's plenty to be scared of in Doctor Sleep: prior ghosts still haunting from the Overlook, the new antagonists of elderly True Knot, and even the twelve steps of Alcoholics Anonymous. But what resounds true to me is King's ability to write the truth of life, the importance of family, and what matters most in the end.<br /><br />EDIT: Thanks to Randall for correcting me. I misspelled The Shining as The Shinning; I've corrected that with this edit.

B0031RSA24|4.0|This book affirmed many of my views on the topic of religion, and I found myself parting company with the author on very, very, few things. I have been an atheist (though not an Atheist) for some time now. I learned long ago not to mention this in polite company (only speaking of your sexual proclivities or preferences is south of saying you don't think there is a gracious old man peering down from the clouds at you all the time, it seems). I also learnt long ago not to debate religion with believers because quite frankly, I fear that shortly after I've &#34;proven&#34; their belief to be a false reality I will find myself running away from pitchforks and torches, moving house (possibly country) and adopting a secret identity. All this I really cannot afford. Yet.<br /><br />Somehow it is more comforting to all concerned if I declare yourself an Agnostic. Each camp is then secure in the knowledge I am not calling them out, and mutter under their breath that sooner or later I will see the light and be saved/ enlightened. Well. This book struck me as far braver than I have been until now, professing to atheism in safe, secure salons with friends, and pretending to agnosticism in the public square. So much for that!<br /><br />The caricature of the 'rabid Atheist' is often applied to Professor Dawkins, and as far as I am concerned it is unjust. Reading this book reveals that his is a much more nuanced world-view. He does not (for example) discount the literary value of scripture, given how references to its phrases, allegories, philosophical dialogue, and parables inform all human literature. However, he echoes a private thought of my own when he affirms that this does not elevate the Bible or the Quran or the Gita above, say, Tolkein, or Erikson, or the Marvel and DC Multi-verses.<br /><br />The book was a challenging read for me because many of the early chapters were relatively 'dry' and convoluted, as he goes into each major argument for the existence of god and, having proactively anticipated every possible supporting assertion, proceeds to argue strenuously against each one. This makes for a tortuous read. He also has a highly irritating tendency to drop references to his other works (no mate, I haven't read all your preceding works nor have the time to) as well as to several other books and authors helpfully listed in the back of the book for reading at leisure. In an example of the pot calling the kettle black, let me also assert that he constructs some really complex sentences that are painful to read.<br /><br />That said, once he gets past the early chapters he hits an almost Sagan like stride, and the prose flows. He's at his strident best when critiquing scripture or when calling spades, spades.<br /><br />If religion has four 'uses' as he suggests - explanation (of the origin, purpose, and eventual end of the universe), exhortation (of morality), consolation (to those in need of succor), and inspiration, he spends far too much time on the first two. Yes, clearly those two are the focus of most organized religion, and it is the religion apologists that resort to the latter two. As far as I'm concerned though, anyone (yes, I'll say it) stupid enough to think the Earth is 6000 years old, or that there was once a monkey god who devoured the Sun, or that women must never reveal more than their hands and eyes is beyond help. This is the ignorant person that scares me with violence, and the less I have to do with him the better. The more 'enlightened' believers (oxymoron, much?) are the ones that deserve convincing and persuasion, and they go to the latter two. For them religion may not be 'truth' but it is 'valuable'. That, to my mind should've been the target audience for this book. Still, I can understand going for the low hanging fruit that is the absurdity of religions cosmogony and eschatology.<br /><br />I would heartily recommend this book to believers and non-believers alike, as a must read. Ultimately (and cynically) though, I don't think it will make a whit of difference. Those that live with faith will find reasons to not trust science, or find intrinsic value in religion, and stick to their faith; those that are without will do just fine as well.<br /><br />This said, I'm glad I read this book for the anecdotes, for the reasoning, and for the references to many other useful books and scientific literature. I promise this review is as far as I'll go in professing my own lack of belief as well, for my own comfort and for that of others. This is me, going back into my unbelieving shell now. Thanks for listening...lly) though, I don't think it will make a whit of difference. Those that live with faith will find reasons to not trust science, or find intrinsic value in religion, and stick to their faith; those that are without will do just fine as well.<br /><br />This said, I'm glad I read this book for the anecdotes, for the reasoning, and for the references to many other useful books and scientific literature. B00E6895M6|3.0|Glasgow electronic pop trio Chvrches have been steadily gaining attention over the past year thanks to a slew of catchy singles and their Recover EP earlier this March, and the anticipation has reached a fever pitch thanks to the release of their debut album, The Bones of What You Believe. They've captured hearts and minds so far by presenting exuberant and addicting synth pop, and their full length offers very little deviations from the sound hinted at by their early singles.<br /><br />They aren't dark and suffocating like Crystal Castles; they make no effort to be highly experimental like The Knife, and they certainly aren't downright crazy like Grimes. They utilize an extreme back to basics approach, presenting their sound as simply and as straightforward as possible. For this reason, Chvrches will likely come across as very basic and elementary to anyone who's been listening to this style of music for awhile. However, the thrust of their success so far has not been so much due to ideology as it has been to the execution itself.<br /><br />This album is filled with very cute, happy and uplifting music carried by the always spirited and adorable voice of Lauren Mayberry. With her high pitched, sweet and sugary voice, she's naturally going to draw comparisons to Megan James of Purity Ring. Yet Chrvches sound is much simpler, as they make an effort to put everything right on the surface. In many ways, it sounds like an album geared toward teenagers or high school age kids with its twinkling rave backgrounds and its themes focusing on interpersonal relationships.<br /><br />Yet the passion and the level of earnestness with which they present the material make it accessible to a wide variety of age ranges.Some lyrics have a definite bite to them. On \\"Gun,\\" Mayberry declares herself to be a gun, and it's you she'll be coming for. Some low pitched gurgling electronica running under the verses adds to the song's confrontational style, while still keeping with the album's by maintaining a general upbeat nature.<br /><br />\\"Tether,\\" meanwhile, is a slow gushing ballad that sounds like a breakup anthem, complete with some spacey guitar strums that washes over the listener like waves in the sea. Most songs begin simply enough, and then add layer upon layer, building as it goes until it ends with a nice finale. It's predicable song structuring, but still remains effective. Although the sound is heavily keyboard based, every now and then you can hear a little bit of guitar which brings in some very minor new wave/post punk influence. \\"Night Sky\\" is bolstered by an 80s inspired surf guitar sound, while \\"Lies\\" sounds something like U2 doing New Wave.<br /><br />To be sure, this is not a particularly deep record, nor does it bring much new to the table. As Iain Cook said to Pitchfork's Rob Cohen, the band aims for their melody to be \\"up front and immediate.\\" This draws the listener in easily on the first few playthrough, but leaves little to discover on repeat listens. Therefore, The Bones of What You Believe is best consumed by those in need of a soundtrack for a party or club type setting, or just anyone who wants to have a good time and not take their music too seriously.

B00008PW3Q|5.0|As many have noted, \\"Say You Will\\" sounds like a Lindsey Buckingham solo album stitched together with a Stevie Nicks solo album and released as a Fleetwood Mac album.  Not to fret: Fleetwood Mac was never much of a collaborative effort to begin with - even the venerable \\"Tusk\\" was once described by Mick Fleetwood as sounding like three solo albums stitched together. (Alas, that's just not possible any longer since the departure of Christine McVie.)  Once more, not to fret: McVie's absence doesn't move the needle here - there's more than enough (18 songs!) material here and the songwriting is strong throughout.<br /><br />In particular, this is a stunning batch of tunes from Lindsey Buckingham.  These are quirky, guitar powered gems that remind the listener that no one can touch Buckingham when he's as on top of his game as he is here.  (It's fair to note that his game has gotten decidedly odder over the years - those intent on hearing him re-hash Go Your Own Way need not spend any time with this set.)  For those willing to take the trip on LB's terms, however, it's quite a ride.  From one of his most beautiful songs (Bleed To Love Her) to one of his ugliest (Come), Buckingham covers astonishing ground here and his playing (both electric and acoustic) is riveting throughout.  \\"Miranda\\" features a fabulous little electric solo and Red Rover demonstrates just what Buckingham can do with an acoustic guitar in hand. As a special bonus, for those interested in rock music as the direct expression of the intersection of male ego and male sexuality, Come is probably the ne plus ultra.  These lyrics lay bare Buckingham's gargantuan ego; once he's bedded a lady, no other man will ever satisfy her.  (And I thought that was just me!) Offensive in precisely the way rock music ought to be offensive, the song also features an incredible, extended electric solo from Buckingham.<br /><br />Stevie Nicks fares a bit less well, despite a pretty strong batch of songs.  In particular, I'dargue that Thrown Down ranks right up there  with her best work and that the title track is one of those instant hits that she whips off from time to time.  The issue with her half of the album lies more in the fussy production that buries some of her better melodies under the weight of drum loops and needlessly thick arrangements.  Some, like Smile At You and Destiny Rules, are largely spared the heavy hand, others, like Illume, get a rougher ride.  Maybe they're not always tracked at their highest potential, but, if you can \\"listen thru\\" the hash, this group of songs represents a strong effort from Stevie Nicks.<br /><br />At the end of the day, \\"Say You Will\\" surprises and delights.  It's absolutely a must have for Buckingham die-hards, and a worthwhile addition to the collection of any Fleetwood Mac fan.  As more of a Buckingham kind of guy, it's in near constant rotation and I have a strange feeling that this one might well end up my favorite FM album.  Like all of FM's best work, it challenges the listener to reconcile that which has been hopelessly torn asunder.  Go ahead and enjoy Stevie Nicks' mystical romanticism snugged up to Lindsey Buckingham's brutal cynicism.  The music is at once comfortably mainstream and restlessly experimental.  It's less coherent than Rumours, and less gloriously incoherent than Tusk.  Nevertheless, \\"Say You Will\\" proudly walks a magical middle ground that may eventually prove even more satisfying than either of those FM classics.

B00D6DVSAE|5.0|It has been ten long years since the gifted singer-songwriter Sting has released a CD with new material and I am glad to report he is in superb form.  \\"The Last Ship\\" consists of songs written for a new musical set to debut on Broadway in 2014.  In recent years there have been several attempts by contemporary artists to crossover to Broadway. These efforts proved disastrous for U2 with the notorious \\"Spider-Man Turn Off the Dark\\" and for Paul Simon with \\" The Capeman\\" (though I still love the score). But Sting's work is the finest crossover effort I have heard since Duncan Sheik's \\"Spring Awakening\\" score which won him a well-deserved Tony Award.  Inspired by Sting's previous album Soul Cages, these songs illuminate the world of a dying British shipyard in the 1980's and this is clearly a labor of love for Sting who grew up in the shadows of the Newcastle shipyards.<br />One of Sting's gifts as an artist has always been his ability  to seamlessly meld disparate musical elements into a rich unified musical tapestry. This piece draws from Celtic folk music, traditional shanties, world music and jazz.If you are open to hearing Sting bring his unique musical sensibilities to a more theatrical presentation I highly recommend this CD.  Here is my take on each song:<br />1)The Last Ship: This haunting waltz with its poetically evocative lyric is a powerful opener, an ominous song marking the death knell of the British shipbuilding industry. Sting's singing on this piece is especially heartfelt and effectively dramatic.<br />2)Dead Man's Boots: What begins as a tender lullaby sung by a father to his son transforms into a rock ballad sung by the rebellious son, rejecting the traditions his father had hoped to leave him as a legacy. Builds to a powerful finish.<br />3)And Yet: A seductive bossa nova feel perfectly captures the ambivalence of the son being pulled back to his childhood seaside home. Sting is singing with tremendous vigor. A favorite cut.<br />4)August Winds: Accompanied by accordion and acoustic guitar this lushly lyrical song conveys the power the sea holds over the town's inhabitants. A gorgeous vocal by Sting.<br />5)Language of Birds:A hybrid of traditional Celtic music with propulsive world beat music that beautifully captures the difficult nature of life as a shipbuilder. Another great lyric.<br />6)Practical Arrangement: My favorite cut hands down. This jazz inflected ballad is harmonically reminiscent of the music of musical theater genius Stephen Sondheim. The conversational lyric is quite poignant.<br />7)The Night the Pugilist Learned How To Dance: This waltz is very simple musically. It is more of a character piece with a man establishing how he won over the woman who became his wife. However the lyric is very clever. A more overtly theatrical piece.<br />8)Ballad of the Great Eastern: A traditional Celtic ballad that begins as a celebration of the construction of a great ship then turns elegiac as it turns into a tragic tale of lives lost.<br />9)What Have We Got: My least favorite cut. An up-tempo choral number sung by the ship-builders that may work wonderfully on stage but not as successfully on disc. Features Jimmy Nail.<br />10)I Love Her: This piece is a sequel to Practical Arrangement. Another haunting ballad bolstered by a heartrending lyric.<br />11)So To Speak: A medium tempo ode to eternal love that does a beautiful job of incorporating the metaphor of a ship that has sailed. One of Sting's best lyrics. Duet with Becky Uthank.<br />12)The Last Ship-Reprise: An ironic, sardonic commentary on how British society has changed in the past few decades.<br />Kudos for Sting for creating this ambitious heartfelt work!companied by accordion and acoustic guitar this lushly lyrical song conveys the power the sea holds over the town's inhabitants. B00000JMOF|5.0|Universal's \\"Frankenstein\\" (1931) has become the most iconic and powerful vision of the Frankenstein story in modern culture. Some of that is well deserved from an historical film perspective, but it comes at a cost.<br /><br />It is hard to believe that anyone today who is over the age of 20 does not know this film from its many showings on TV. It's a standard of Turner Classics, and in times past, was often brought out on \\"Creature Feature\\" Saturday matinee TV shows and their ilk. The film was shown in the late 1950's on TV, and it has been on TV many times since.<br /><br />It comes at a cost because it is completely unfaithful to the famous novel by Mary Shelly, and it lacks the nuance and pathos that are hallmarks of the novel. So to watch this film and believe you are getting the story as it was intended by the author is an exercise in frustration.<br /><br />As an historical film, however, it was, and is groundbreaking. By looking at the history, we can understand it.<br /><br />Universal had a smash hit in 1931 with \\"Dracula\\", starring Bela Lugosi. They wanted to continue their success in the horror genre and Frankenstein, probably the most iconic monster story of the 19th century next to Dracula, was an obvious choice.<br /><br />At the time, \\"talkies\\" were a very new technology, and even movies were a new medium. Film had yet to truly come into its own, so it had to borrow from other venues, and the closest one was of course, the stage.<br /><br />Dracula, the movie, was based on an extremely popular stage play (in which Lugosi portrayed the main character). He was perfect for the part on stage, being tall, suave and sophisticated... the perfect image of a Romanian count of ancient heritage. He was hit on stage, and carried it over very successfully to the film.<br /><br />Universal wanted him to play the Frankenstein monster, but he refused because it wasn't a speaking part, and the script would have limited him far too much. If he had been the monster, the movie might have failed, because he would have been coming at it from the success of Dracula and it would have been a step down for him. That would have come across in the movie.<br /><br />It was fortune (or fate) that landed Karloff, a relative unknown, in the part, and against all odds, he pulled off a \\"save\\" for the movie (he is the most beloved and well remembered character from the movie). In spite of the incredible makeup by Jack Pierce (which took about 5 hours to apply), the stiff costume (he had to wear three pairs of heavy pants so he would not be 'flexible'), and the short scenes (he doesn't appear all that often actually) and lack of dialog, Karloff made the character his own and set a record of sorts. From then on, the image and character of Frankenstein's monster was entirely based on his performance, and his career in film, especially horror films was assured. Ironically, as horrible as Franklenstin's monster was in the movie, Karloff himself was a very handsome man, and by all reports a 'class act', being kind and gentle, with a beautiful speaking voice. Many reviewers have attributed the strange attraction audiences have for this version of Frankenstein due to Karloff's own personality shining through the makeup and awkward unyielding script.<br /><br />In my opinion, the success of the film rides on Karloff's shoulders. I cannot imagine it working as well with any other actor.<br /><br />The script to this movie has some enormous illogical holes in it by today's, or any other standards. When the monster appears, he walks backwards through a door. This is illogical, of course, but a great way to build suspense until he turns around for the 'grand reveal' and half the movie audience screams in terror and shock. Remember in the 1930's no one had seen anything like this makeup. Even Lon Chaney might not have come up with it. It was groundbreaking (and later, heavily copyrighted).<br /><br />The screenplay was based on a stage play that was based onShelly's novel. I don't remember if the play actually was performed anywhere, but if it had been, it would have joined a long line of stage plays based on the novel, all of which changed the story to meet the needs of the stage. Shelly herself had attended some of the plays and it is reported that she found them 'amusing'.<br /><br />The novel, Frankenstein, is very complex and psychological, perfectly suited to reading, but very difficult to dramatize on stage or screen. It doesn't have a lot of action, and relies more on the relationship of the characters and their dialog to move the reader.<br /><br />So it must be kept in mind that when Universal made this film, they were following the \\"best practices\\" of the time, using the stage as their role model. It wasn't for many years that film screenplays took true advantage of the power of film in its own right and brought it to the high level of an art form that we enjoy today.<br /><br />Another thing to consider is that this was an early \\"talkie\\". This explains the tendency to over-act (especially Colin Clive's portrayal of Henry Frankenstein). The silent film actors used grandiose gestures and expressions to display emotion and motivation. Clive and the others of the cast came from this tradition, so it is natural that they would bring it into this film. No one at the time fully understood the importance that sound was bringing to film because it was so new. Note that there is no musical score even, except at the beginning and the end. Consider how since the time of this movie, music has been used to cue the audience in on what is going on. It was even used in the silent movies when the theater organ was used to provide a musical accompaniment to the action. Somehow, Universal didn't bring this to either Dracula or Frankenstein, although we do get the thunder and rain effects.<br /><br />Much has been said in praise of James Whale's direction of the film, so I won't address that here, other than to say much of it was innovative, as were the elaborate laboratory sets (none of which was in the novel).<br /><br />This film should not be viewed in comparison to modern movies, nor in comparison to the book. It just doesn't work that way. Instead, it should be viewed in comparison to what came before. In such a light, you can see what advancements it made in the world of cinema.<br /><br />And, if you must, compare it to the earlier Dracula. Admittedly, the character of Dracula is much nicer to look at that Frankenstein's monster, but Frankenstein's monster beat him out over time, for the image from this film is indelibly imprinted on the popular culture. In spite of Universal's very strict copyright protections - the monster makeup has been imitated over and over again, and the character created by Karloff still has that eerie appeal almost a century later.<br /><br />Could it have been a better film? Judging by what we can do today, of course. But judging by what they could do back then, their budget, and what was being done back then, probably not.<br /><br />So enjoy the film for what it is, the way it is. It certainly has stood the test of time.Shelly's novel. I don't remember if the play actually was performed anywhere, but if it had been, it would have joined a long line of stage plays based on the novel, all of which changed the story to meet the needs of the stage. Shelly herself had attended some of the plays and it is reported that she found them 'amusing'.<br /><br />The novel, Frankenstein, is very complex and psychological, perfectly suited to reading, but very difficult to dramatize on stage or screen. It doesn't have a lot of action, and relies more on the relationship of the characters and their dialog to move the reader.<br /><br />So it must be kept in mind that when Universal made this film, they were following the \\"best practices\\" of the time, using the stage as their role model. It wasn't for many years that film screenplays took true advantage of the power of film in its own right and brought it to the high level of an art form that we enjoy today.<br /><br />Another thing to consider is that this was an early \\"talkie\\". This explains the tendency to over-act (especially Colin Clive's portrayal of Henry Frankenstein). The silent film actors used grandiose gestures and expressions to display emotion and motivation. Clive and the others of the cast came from this tradition, so it is natural that they would bring it into this film. No one at the time fully understood the importance that sound was bringing to film because it was so new. Note that there is no musical score even, except at the beginning and the end. Consider how since the time of this movie, music has been used to cue the audience in on what is going on. It was even used in the silent movies when the theater organ was used to provide a musical accompaniment to the action. Somehow, Universal didn't bring this to either Dracula or Frankenstein, although we do get the thunder and rain effects.<br /><br />Much has been said in praise of James Whale's direction of the film, so I won't address that here, other than to say much of itwas innovative, as were the elaborate laboratory sets (none of which was in the novel).<br /><br />This film should not be viewed in comparison to modern movies, nor in comparison to the book. It just doesn't work that way. Instead, it should be viewed in comparison to what came before. In such a light, you can see what advancements it made in the world of cinema.<br /><br />And, if you must, compare it to the earlier Dracula. Admittedly, the character of Dracula is much nicer to look at that Frankenstein's monster, but Frankenstein's monster beat him out over time, for the image from this film is indelibly imprinted on the popular culture. In spite of Universal's very strict copyright protections - the monster makeup has been imitated over and over again, and the character created by Karloff still has that eerie appeal almost a century later.<br /><br />Could it have been a better film? Judging by what we can do today, of course. But judging by what they could do back then, their budget, and what was being done back then, probably not.<br /><br />So enjoy the film for what it is, the way it is. It certainly has stood the test of time.

B002RUA524|5.0|WARNING: this review has spoilers<br /><br />...<br /><br />I was in the middle of Lolita and Rebecca, dark, intense books both, when I came down with a fever, probably caused by those two depressing narrators. So I set about hunting for something lighter and zeroed in on My Family and Other Animals, and proceeded to spend most of my illness surrounded by cushions, drinking tea and alternately grinning and laughing.<br /><br />At first I thought My Family and Other Animals was a simple book, full of \\"swifties\\" and indifferent expressions, not written to enlighten or even impress. Everyone says something \\"unguardedly\\" or \\"suspiciously\\" or something-else-ly. It has the freshness of a hand-written letter, as if the author did not bother to edit or improve anything once it was written down. I could not have found something more unlike Lolita, which incidentally I believe is a grand work of art that has to be studied, annotated and preserved with reverence.<br /><br />Ostensibly, My Family and Other Animals is the story of the narrator's exploits with the animals on the Greek island of Corfu. In truth, it is a book without a plot, consisting of various things that happened to cross the author's lively, extraordinary head. If I came across a description such as that one, I'd immediately blacklist the book, but luckily for me I did not read the reviews.<br /><br />Various Greek characters come and go throughout the story, in appearance and character as distinct and colorful as the countless animals that inhabit the island. Theodore, a well-known botanist, goes wandering across the island with Gerry the twelve year old narrator, subjecting the spiders and the beetles to a thoughtful, absent-minded scrutiny that masks an obsession. Trapdoor spiders lie waiting beneath tiny, devious trapdoors for their prey. The rose-beetle man sings funny songs and sells various pets - over-friendly tortoises and musical parrots - to Gerry after long haggling over the prices. Oil-beetle larvae hitchhike on a special bee's fur, disembark surreptitiously on the bee's cell (which contains the egg), eat the poor bee's egg and grow into adulthood inside the cell.<br /><br />The titular family of five, who according to the author \\"having got themselves on paper, then proceeded to establish themselves and invite various friends to share the chapters,\\" is led by a somewhat hapless Mother with whom I instantly fell in love. She is no match for the guile of even her youngest son, Gerry, and is led on a series of hilarious misadventures by her eccentric children and the overprotective and very British family dog, Roger. Roger shares every one of Gerry's zoological trips, inspecting and compiling the lavish fauna of the island with a zeal matched only by his youthful owner. There is also a boat, very dignified and rotund, but carrying the unfortunate name of Bootle-Bumtrinket.<br /><br />Over the years, it has become increasingly harder for me to find a book that leaves an impression -- a book that provokes thought or laughter, shines a blazing light on an unspeakable horror, conjures wonderful ideas, metaphors and landscapes, or incites a bout of feverish soul-searching. My Family and Other Animals is an exquisite exception that fills you with the same irrepressible joy and curiosity that seems to rule Gerry. It forces one to suspend not one's disbelief, but one's skepticism, a much rarer gift to a reader. Only a few pages in, I was already half-convinced that nothing destructive existed anywhere in the universe. All of earth suddenly seemed to be laid out like a buffet, promising unsuspected delights and mysteries, awaiting one's endless examination, just as the island of Corfu had laid waiting for Gerry Durell to poke and prod anything that showed an inclination to move when he wasn't lifting barks to peer underneath in case a precious dung-beetle had gotten left out.

They have a LITTLE energy to them and some melody but we all know, we'll never hear them in concert a couple years from now.<br /><br />And that's the problem with Elton--he may be writing what he wants, but we all know he'd like some more monster hits because when he tours, he never plays anything from BIG PICTURE, et al--he goes back to his greatest hits, His last really well written/produced album was THE ONE with a great variety of songs.<br /><br />If he records another CD, I would love the record contract to state that he MUST write 5 upbeat, pop-rock songs. 1476727651|2.0|Imagine Shirley Jackson writing a sequel to THE HAUNTING OF HILL HOUSE without the house, or Richard Matheson's HELL HOUSE sequel sans the Belasco mansion?<br /><br />That's one of the many, many, many things that are wrong with Stephen King's sequel to THE SHINING--the Overlook hotel never makes an appearance since it was destroyed by fire (in the book, not the movie.) The whole premise of THE SHINING was a haunted hotel and a boy with a special gift.<br /><br />What we have in DOCTOR SLEEP (really dull title, BTW) is the further adventures of Danny Torrance. Problem is, if you read the novel, Danny was never a very interesting character--his father was and the hotel was. But in this sequel, we are stuck with Danny.<br /><br />And Danny drinks--a lot.<br /><br />And goes to a LOT of AA meetings; and we go with him.<br /><br />To. Every. Single. One.<br /><br />I'm talking dozens of pages of AA meetings that add zero to the book. King struggled with alcoholism during the early years of his career and one reason he so disliked Kubrick's film was because that aspect of Jack Torrance was significantly excised from the film. So King wrote it back in when he did his 1997 TV miniseries. So alcoholism played--and plays--a big role in the characters of THE SHINING and DOCTOR SLEEP.<br /><br />But it doesn't result in very interesting characters. In fact, this is the first book of King's (I've read more than 40 of them) where his skill with characters is absent. There are no characters in this book, just names on a page--and pages you will be frantically skimming by the time you reach page 200 because you will be desperately look for a plot to grab you and suck you into the story.<br /><br />Like everyone else reading this review, I was SO EXCITED to read DOCTOR SLEEP. (I was given a galley.)<br /><br />This sequel to one of my favorite King novels came with great expectations, and he even said it would be a return to real horror. (\\"Basically, the idea of the story was to try and scare the s--t out of people!\\" he said)<br /><br />But here's the bad news: IT'S NOT SCARY. At all.<br /><br />The supernatural evil force Danny deals with is someone called \\"Nan the Hat.\\" Seriously. I'm not making it up. And Nan and her minions aren't even really supernatural--they can be destroyed by measles. And shotguns. Yes, seriously.<br /><br />By page 400 of the more than 600 page galley, I knew it was a real disaster. Of course, \\"everyone\\" will buy it but I can't honestly imagine many will give it a good review. There is no Overlook Hotel. There are no scares. There are no classic King characters.<br /><br />In fact, the last 200 pages read like a young adult adventure novel. If it wasn't pitched as a sequel, it would still be a bad book, and it borrows heavily from IT and THE DEAD ZONE.<br /><br />This was a huge disappointment for me. If anyone likes/loves it, I look forward to reading your reviews and comments.<br /><br />And please provide some examples of what you found so scary or compelling about the story.<br /><br />It's well known that King postponed publication of DOCTOR SLEEP from January 2013 to fall 2013 since he admitted it needed some serious editing.<br /><br />If only he had postponed it again or better yet, shelved an idea that clearly doesn't work.

031623480X|5.0|I was ecstatic to hear that my husband won a free Advance Reading Copy of Grain Brain through GoodReads.  The author, David Perlmutter, primarily focuses on the neurological affects grain--especially wheat--can have on the body. Many studies have shown that the #1 cause of dementia is from a gluten-filled, high carb diet. Perlmutter goes above and beyond in his book by also debunking several food myths: 1) That fat is bad for you, 2) High cholesterol is dangerous and must be avoided, and 3) People should consume high amounts of grains and carbohydrates each day. Perlmutter explains how these myths only contribute to neurological disorders, while studies have shown that elderly people who consumed high amounts of fat, fewer carbohydrates, and had a higher cholesterol level were overall healthier than those who followed the opposite kind of diet.<br /><br />When you think about it, there has to be a reason why diabetes, dementia, obesity, and various cardiovascular and neurological problems have been on the rise in the past 30 years, and I believe--as mentioned in Grain Brain- that a lot of these issues are contributed to our diet changes.<br /><br />I had made the decision to give up grain and most carbohydrates in February of 2012. I experienced a radical change just as many of Perlmutter's patients had experienced. Since then, I have incorporated some foods back into my diet, so long as they did not contain gluten or corn. Reading Perlmutter's Grain Brain has made me rethink my consumption of grains and certain carbs for the sake of my neurological health, and I wholeheartedly recommend this book to anyone who desires to see change in their health. Gothika, with its creepy cover seemed to fit the bill.<br /><br />Miranda Grey is a psychologist.  She works at a Penitentiary in the psych ward.  After driving home and having an odd occurrence though, she wakes up only to find herself a patient in the same Penitentiary.  Worst thing is, everyone seems to believe she has committed murder, and something unnatural is stalking her.  But since she is now \\"crazy\\" she has no one to turn to for help.<br /><br />Characters<br />Halle Berry: Miranda Drey<br />Robert Downey Jr: Pete Graham<br />Charles Dutton: Douglas Grey<br />John Carroll Lynch: Sheriff Ryan<br />Penelope Cruz: Chloe Sava<br />Bernard Hill: Phil Parsons<br /><br />For as many famous actors that are in this movie, they are definitely under-utilized.  Berry gets the most screen time, and given that's she's supposed to be crazy for the larger part of the movie, that's definitely interesting.  But I think she does more screaming than talking.  Cruz probably has the next biggest role, and since she's an inmate too, again, there's not much meaningful dialogue going on.  Although she plays a convincing patient.  Downey Jr. barely has any time at all and I honestly didn't see the point in his part.  He doesn't add much to the movie at all or really develop any breakthroughs.  The Sheriff plays a more important role than him.  It's just weird that more time wasn't spent developing all these characters if they were going to have big names play them.<br /><br />And the plot.  Well, let's just say you have to suspend disbelief for a lot of it.  And I'm not talking about the paranormal aspect of it.  To believe that Grey can go from being a doctor to a patient in the same place, within three days, just doesn't make sense.  It seems more logical that she would be in a hospital or other short-term facility rather than the penitentiary.  I liked the overall theme of being part crazy and part truthful and having noone believe you because you're in a psych ward though.  To me, that's more terrifying than the ghosts.  So much could happen to you and noone would believe you because you're in the facility.  You just have to hope the wardens aren't corrupt or sadistic.<br /><br />The overall scene was dark and shadowy.  It made me wonder why the facility didn't do something about their electric.  Especially when dealing with people with fragile psyches.  I know it was just the movie trying to be scary, but it was another unbelievable thing.  But it did set the tone for this being a horror film.  As did the special effects.  They were pretty well done and realistic and it seemed as if Grey was not in control of her own body when the paranormal was attacking her.  To cap it all off, we have \\"Behind Blue Eyes\\" as the song that plays at the end of the film (not much music otherwise) and to me that's always been a depressing song so it fits.<br /><br />Not a great film but it's somewhat spooky.  If you like big name actors and decent special effects you'll tolerate it.  But if you're expecting spectacular writing and dialogue, this just isn't the movie for it.<br /><br />Review by M.
